<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13901259321289861506">"It can wear you to a nub, trying to be a popular person and a good Catholic all at the same time." So says Sidda, one of the characters inhabiting <em>Little Altars Everywhere</em>. Author Rebecca Wells uses her considerable acting talent to perform this abridgment, adding even more spark to her already lively characters. Everyone--Shep, Vivi, Willetta, and the rest--is given a di</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13901259321289861506">"It can wear you to a nub, trying to be a popular person and a good Catholic all at the same time." So says Sidda, one of the characters inhabiting <em>Little Altars Everywhere</em>. Author Rebecca Wells uses her considerable acting talent to perform this abridgment, adding even more spark to her already lively characters. Everyone--Shep, Vivi, Willetta, and the rest--is given a distinct voice, and Wells plays each of them to the hilt. More like a recording of a one-woman show than a mere reading, <em>Altars</em> is an excellent example of how entertaining audiobooks can be. <em>--C.B. Delaney</em></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_58549395">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58549395" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2281833-ami" class="left imgcol" title="Ami"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1388773624p2/2281833.jpg" alt="Ami" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/58549395?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 25, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2281833-ami" class="user" title="Ami">Ami</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2281833-ami?shelf=contemporary-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">contemporary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer58549395">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6052627425986548595">This book leaves a bad taste in my mouth and a hole in my heart.<br /><br />I have previously read the other two books in the Ya-Ya series, and upon completion I felt Vivi Walker was a damaged woman who sincerely tried to do the best she could with the hand she was dealt. "Ya-Yas in Bloom," in particular, ended with a feeling of redemption for the entire Walker clan. However, after reading "Little Altars Everywhere," I am disgusted beyond belief at this character. The Vivi Walker in this book is a bitter, a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6052627425986548595">This book leaves a bad taste in my mouth and a hole in my heart.<br /><br />I have previously read the other two books in the Ya-Ya series, and upon completion I felt Vivi Walker was a damaged woman who sincerely tried to do the best she could with the hand she was dealt. "Ya-Yas in Bloom," in particular, ended with a feeling of redemption for the entire Walker clan. However, after reading "Little Altars Everywhere," I am disgusted beyond belief at this character. The Vivi Walker in this book is a bitter, angry, cruel child molester who ruins the lives of her children. Forever. I am not able, for the life of me, to understand how other critics and readers can describe this book as "hilarious" or "witty" or "nostalgic."<br /><br />Overall, this book leaves me feeling conned. I fell for the Ya-Yas (Vivi especially) only to discover that at their core they are rotten. I am unable to reconcile the two images I now have for this family and that leaves me feeling angry and betrayed.<br /><br />Take my advice, read the other Ya-Ya books, but little "Little Altars" alone.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6052627425986548595">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_172961369">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172961369" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/752176-vanessa" class="left imgcol" title="Vanessa"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1199757927p2/752176.jpg" alt="Vanessa" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/172961369?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 09, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/752176-vanessa" class="user" title="Vanessa">Vanessa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/752176-vanessa?shelf=read-in-2011" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer172961369">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8525735814999896855">I almost want to say there is something Proustian about this novel except while I don't fear intellectual eye-rolling over my calling a popular novel written by and about southern women Proustian, I do fear eye-rolling over not quite correct use of the word. What I mean, then, is reading this novel was a gorgeously vivid sensory experience. When the Walker kids went to swim in the pond, I saw and felt and smelled it like I was in that same summertime water. I felt the cool concrete floors of the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8525735814999896855">I almost want to say there is something Proustian about this novel except while I don't fear intellectual eye-rolling over my calling a popular novel written by and about southern women Proustian, I do fear eye-rolling over not quite correct use of the word. What I mean, then, is reading this novel was a gorgeously vivid sensory experience. When the Walker kids went to swim in the pond, I saw and felt and smelled it like I was in that same summertime water. I felt the cool concrete floors of the grocery store beneath my 10 year old feet. I experienced the vertiginous but exhilarating displacement of wandering through the house of a dimly known adult my parents were visiting looking for food, places to play, and random objects to pick up and examine all the while wondering, "Am I allowed to be in this room?"<br /><br />All of this sensory overload sits on the surface of this loose collection of stories about the Walker clan of Thornton, Louisiana (a prequel of sorts to <a title="Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood by Rebecca Wells" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/137791.Divine_Secrets_of_the_Ya_Ya_Sisterhood">Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood</a> however they can be read in any order.) However, as you read you begin to see the darkness below the surface troubling the waters. Siddha is oddly obsessed with religion and suffering. Lulu pulls out her hair and eats it. Baylor has trouble chewing and swallowing his food. Their mother, Vivi, sure drinks a lot and by the first time someone mentions her hands shaking in the morning, you know. As the story flashes forward midway through from the 60's to the 90's and expands to include the Walker's hired help, the picture grows clearer and darker. And yet even as unlikable as some characters in the book are, you still feel empathy for them even when you can't forgive their behavior. As a case in point, the final story narrated by Vivi, "Looking for My Mules", made me connect and feel sorry for her even when I should have been saying, "Bitch you brought this on yourself." There's a lot of deceptive depth to this kind of writing. And the story is really about much more than just one family's troubles: small town politics, the death of the rural way of life, war, the burden of secrets. <br /><br />The final chapter belongs to Siddha and is a great way to wrap up the story although I wanted to keep going (too bad the reviews of <u>YaYa's in Bloom</u> are all so negative.) Don't hit the baby. Fine advice, indeed. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8525735814999896855">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_509483879">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/509483879" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9040328-stevie" class="left imgcol" title="Stevie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1341295232p2/9040328.jpg" alt="Stevie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/509483879?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 19, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9040328-stevie" class="user" title="Stevie">Stevie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer509483879">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16942206149221972755">Consider this a review of all three of the Ya-ya books because what I have to say about this one can't be said without referencing (without spoilers, of course) the other two. <br /><br />These books are going to be love/hate for everybody who touches them. So let's get the good out of the way: some people say this series sucks because it's another quirky my-dysfunctional-family series and really, that's misleading. The movie made it into that, sure, but these books are a lot deeper than that. Wells writes</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16942206149221972755">Consider this a review of all three of the Ya-ya books because what I have to say about this one can't be said without referencing (without spoilers, of course) the other two. <br /><br />These books are going to be love/hate for everybody who touches them. So let's get the good out of the way: some people say this series sucks because it's another quirky my-dysfunctional-family series and really, that's misleading. The movie made it into that, sure, but these books are a lot deeper than that. Wells writes characters as people. In one story, the characters are delightfully gray. I mean in one story, all of the characters will be right and wrong simultaneously, caring and aloof, vindicated and unquestionably unforgivable. By writing characters this way, you really get a fuller narrative. These characters could easily be people you know, maybe even people you've met before. And the range of characters and narrations is a delight. Also, I love how well connected all the books are. You learn one detail from a story and it pops up in other stories, from the point of view of others or as a reference or an example. You see these characters from 1st person, 2nd person, and 3rd person, with different people taking on different roles and evolving (for many of them) from childhood to adulthood. My favorite example of this is Mr. Ogden who is an often mentioned character (the least "fun" of the husbands). He starts as a reference, even sometimes as a stereotypical stuck-up older white man, but by book three, we see him as a fearful grandfather, a protective father, and a vulnerable man all in one story. It's a great evolution and it's nice to see characters written this way in short story format. <br /><br />The major problem that readers will face is a difficult one. All people triggered by child abuse or alcoholism, stay away. Do not even bother with this. And that is part of the problem. In Little Altars Everywhere, we find out that Vivian abuses her children above and beyond "hey, it's the 1960s and everybody hits children as a form of discipline". If you've seen the movie, it is much worse in the book and, in one story, we find out as an audience that Vivian sexually abused one of her children, the extent of which is vague. This issue is never brought up again. Ever. It's in one story from one of her children's point of view and while the story is well written and deals with the honest truth of coming from an abusive home and being unsure of one's body and one's boundaries with others, that's it. One well written story and done. It's never mentioned again in any of the other books. So you, as a reader, see so much about how Vivian has suffered and how she gets into crazy escapades with her girlfriends...all while knowing that she's a child molester and a child beater. And the main book (the one the movie is based on) takes a sinister feel to it when you realize that the Ya-Yas are trying to fix a relationship between a woman and her mother who abused her. Do they excuse her behavior? Yes and no at the same time and it's uncomfortable. But through this exploration of Vivian, we see the cycle of abuse and how it continues from one generation to the next. So many people gush about Vivi as if she is the most awesome thing ever and I think the movie is slightly to blame for that because when you hear her mother's back story and see what happens with her children, it's really hard to sit through her stories. It's hard to empathize with her and it does color many of the stories from her point of view. <br /><br />Does she have a tragic back story that causes everything? Yes and it is heartbreaking to watch how she is nearly broken over and over again. But does it take away from her unapologetic attitude about the abuse? Why does Siddalee need to have her relationship mended with her mother? Why don't her siblings have a similar experience? It puts a sour taste in your mouth and that might be too much for some readers. <br /><br />Also, I'm really not sure how to feel about this but there are at least two (I might be remembering wrong) stories from the point of view of the Walkers' black maid and handyman. Their stories, while a more honest portrayal of black people than you usually get from Southern literature, are still hard to deal with without getting headaches at the stereotypes that Wells doesn't break. I think they're worth reading but bear that in mind, POCs. It's not THE HELP but it's not BELOVED either, okay?<br /><br />I personally like these books because of the development of character and setting, not for the happy fun joy ride with kooky characters that some proclaim that it is. I like it because it's realistic and every happy ending has a black lining through it. If you're curious, check it out but I really cannot stress enough that you go into this series knowing what you're in for. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16942206149221972755">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1774876706765912346">After 100 pages I had to give up on this. Maybe I have a beef with stories about dysfunctional Southern families. No, that's not true. I love Flannery O'Connor. And anyone who perused my book list knows I do not shy away from the darker aspects of life...or from very dark comedies, which I think this is trying to be. Yet Wells seems to think there is something warm and funny about abuse and molestation. The scatter-shot styling of writing and alternating viewpoints dd not help at all to bring an</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1774876706765912346">After 100 pages I had to give up on this. Maybe I have a beef with stories about dysfunctional Southern families. No, that's not true. I love Flannery O'Connor. And anyone who perused my book list knows I do not shy away from the darker aspects of life...or from very dark comedies, which I think this is trying to be. Yet Wells seems to think there is something warm and funny about abuse and molestation. The scatter-shot styling of writing and alternating viewpoints dd not help at all to bring any organization or meaning to this tale. I know a number of people who loved <a title="Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood by Rebecca Wells" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/137791.Divine_Secrets_of_the_Ya_Ya_Sisterhood">Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood</a> but I doubt this "prequel" is adds anything. Perhaps I should try the other book but I'm afraid <em>Little Altars Everywhere</em> has scared me off of Rebecca Wells' novels for good.</span>

              <a href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=fiction-family-drama" class="actionLinkLite">fiction-family-drama</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=fiction-southern" class="actionLinkLite">fiction-southern</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=american" class="actionLinkLite">american</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=received-through-bookcrossing" class="actionLinkLite">received-through-bookcrossing</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=released-through-bookcrossing" class="actionLinkLite">released-through-bookcrossing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer148367574">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11086235345688755309">I think a childhood in America in the 1960's is going to have a lot in common whether you grew up in the suburbs of S. Calif like I did, or in a farming family in a small town in Louisiana like the character Siddalee and her siblings. From being called Sara Bernhardt when you pouted and stomped your foot and whined, to watching Roadrunner on TV, to eating Ritz crackers, bologna sandwiches, Fritos, grilled cheese sandwiches, coca-cola, and snickers bars, calling things "even-steven", being told t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11086235345688755309">I think a childhood in America in the 1960's is going to have a lot in common whether you grew up in the suburbs of S. Calif like I did, or in a farming family in a small town in Louisiana like the character Siddalee and her siblings. From being called Sara Bernhardt when you pouted and stomped your foot and whined, to watching Roadrunner on TV, to eating Ritz crackers, bologna sandwiches, Fritos, grilled cheese sandwiches, coca-cola, and snickers bars, calling things "even-steven", being told to push back your cuticles and wearing cold cream at night. And a lot of the tougher stuff too, like most adults smoking cigarettes and drinking, getting "belted" for a punishment, parents screaming at each other. <br />But this book goes beyond that. It starts out eccentric and charming in its way, the drinking doesn't seem beyond what I used to see, as I said drinking and smoking was accepted in the 1960's as regular behavior, and belting your kids was acceptable as well. This book strays into territory beyond that, it gets kind of monstrous and very sad really. <br />The author is a gifted writer and succeeds at connecting her reader to each character in her story, and to Louisiana itself. Without being overly descriptive each character tells you about what is going on with them at the time and it makes a very complete picture, especially when the children are young. When the children are grown I had a harder time relating to them although I retained my connection to the main four adults: Vivi, Big Shep, Letta and Chaney. I felt especially connected to Big Shep in the section where he finds an old black man in his fields looking for his mules. I could understand his tears and his feelings very well and I mourned the changes and the loss of the farmland in Louisiana, I cried along with Big Shep. Despite Vivi's behavior I found myself often liking her and I liked the way the book wrapped up with Siddalee's declaration to her mother, the one she repeats twice, that felt right. Still a sad book in many ways, not the light-hearted read that I expected. It is much more complex and sophisticated than it would seem to be, a social commentary, not just a story of one family. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11086235345688755309">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_436823260">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/436823260" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5713407-april" class="left imgcol" title="April"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1383247707p2/5713407.jpg" alt="April" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/436823260?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 18, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5713407-april" class="user" title="April">April</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Women, people from the South, </span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5713407-april?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5713407-april?shelf=south" class="actionLinkLite">south</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5713407-april?shelf=race-relations" class="actionLinkLite">race-relations</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5713407-april?shelf=women" class="actionLinkLite">women</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer436823260">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3900858607895313761">Fastest I'v read any book this year! Love, love, love this writer, a cross between Judy Blume and Jodi Picoult. The narrative switches between characters and the plot is built around what each character says and their point of view. For the most part it is told in the present tense, but switches back and forth between the 60s and 90s. The chapters aren't necessarily in chronological order, however, that doesn't diminish the power of the story. This is the introductory book to the famous Divine S</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3900858607895313761">Fastest I'v read any book this year! Love, love, love this writer, a cross between Judy Blume and Jodi Picoult. The narrative switches between characters and the plot is built around what each character says and their point of view. For the most part it is told in the present tense, but switches back and forth between the 60s and 90s. The chapters aren't necessarily in chronological order, however, that doesn't diminish the power of the story. This is the introductory book to the famous Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood. The Ya-Ya's are in the background as we meet and learn about each of the Walker children, parents and help (in the roles of Chaney and Willetta, who I love). Although it is presented in a first person narrative, I didn't get the sense of being "told" something. I really felt and saw the whole picture. Experienced, talented writers can pull this off and believe me it isn't easy to do. I've read a number of "telling" books this year and it is quite exhausting. "Showing" is a fine art, one that takes time to develop. Also, the characters just jump off the page! They are so real. Delve between the covers of Little Altars and prepare to be transported to a different time and place. I didn't grow up in the south (unless you count southern California) but I felt like I connected with these characters and this story. I can't wait to read Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood and finish up with Ya-Ya's in bloom.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3900858607895313761">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_511338658">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/511338658" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6283418-allison-welker" class="left imgcol" title="Allison Welker"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1315876893p2/6283418.jpg" alt="Allison Welker" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/511338658?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 26, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6283418-allison-welker" class="user" title="Allison Welker">Allison Welker</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer511338658">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1547617466096938818"><br />I started this book knowing that it was the first one in the series of the Ya-Ya's. I had read the Devine Secrets a couple of years ago and wanted to start from the beginning, something I'm doing with all of the series' that I have started somewhere in the middle. I loved the Devine Secrets, more than the movie but still just as good. <br /><br />This book goes into not just the life of Siddalee Walker but rather the lives of all of the family member, Vivi, Shep, Little Shep, Lulu, and Baylor as well as the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1547617466096938818"><br />I started this book knowing that it was the first one in the series of the Ya-Ya's. I had read the Devine Secrets a couple of years ago and wanted to start from the beginning, something I'm doing with all of the series' that I have started somewhere in the middle. I loved the Devine Secrets, more than the movie but still just as good. <br /><br />This book goes into not just the life of Siddalee Walker but rather the lives of all of the family member, Vivi, Shep, Little Shep, Lulu, and Baylor as well as the Letta and Chaney, the two people in their lives that were there for them when their parents weren't. If anyone has read the Devine Secrets, they know what kind of life the children were put through. However, I liked seeing it from the point of view of Shep and Vivi too. See how they reacted to the things that happened as well as how it affected their relationship as well. <br /><br />Overall, I'm glad that I read this one second, and not first originally. I think that it gives a great back story to the characters as well as sets up the scene and makes things enjoyable for reading. I thought it was a well written piece and actually want to re-read Devine Secrets as well as read Ya-Ya's in Bloom. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1547617466096938818">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7305851898615389832">Told by all the different voices of this wonderfully witty, funny and troubled southern clan, this was the pre-quel to the Divine Secrets of the Ya Ya Sisterhood. It's a book essentally about relationships. If you fell in love with the Ya Yas like I did, you will appreciate getting to know them better in their younger years and gaining an even better understanding of their undying loyalty. If you appreciated Sidda's dry sarcasm as an adult like I did, then you will enjoy all the little things th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7305851898615389832">Told by all the different voices of this wonderfully witty, funny and troubled southern clan, this was the pre-quel to the Divine Secrets of the Ya Ya Sisterhood. It's a book essentally about relationships. If you fell in love with the Ya Yas like I did, you will appreciate getting to know them better in their younger years and gaining an even better understanding of their undying loyalty. If you appreciated Sidda's dry sarcasm as an adult like I did, then you will enjoy all the little things that contributed to her personalitly as she was growing up--everything from embarassing colors of nail polish to traumatizing girl scouts to of course dealing with her mentally unstable mother. Despite the title, there are definately some earth-shattering events that balance out all the humor. This is called a companion novel to Divine Secrets, but I read it 2nd and was glad I did. For those of you who don't know, the movie was really of combination of the 2 books, and my enjoyment of the books was not diminished in the least by watching the movie. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_542607017">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/542607017" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/710201-skylar-burris" class="left imgcol" title="Skylar Burris"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1449959508p2/710201.jpg" alt="Skylar Burris" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/542607017?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 09, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/710201-skylar-burris" class="user" title="Skylar Burris">Skylar Burris</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/710201-skylar-burris?shelf=literary-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer542607017">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9706277786737138631">The blurb I read about this book billed it as a "novel" and said that it was "funny." Well, it's only funny in the sense, as one of the characters says, of "not funny ha-ha, but funny tired. Funny sad." The book is also more like a short story cycle than a novel. Characters and themes weave together throughout the collection, but each chapter is somewhat self-contained and there are large gaps in time. As such, there wasn't a lot of the sustained tension typically present in a novel, and so I wa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9706277786737138631">The blurb I read about this book billed it as a "novel" and said that it was "funny." Well, it's only funny in the sense, as one of the characters says, of "not funny ha-ha, but funny tired. Funny sad." The book is also more like a short story cycle than a novel. Characters and themes weave together throughout the collection, but each chapter is somewhat self-contained and there are large gaps in time. As such, there wasn't a lot of the sustained tension typically present in a novel, and so I was not compelled to keep reading, despite the fact that the writing itself was good. Whenever I finished a chapter, I thought, "that was well written and affecting," and yet I still didn't feel like I needed to read the next chapter. While I'm glad I read the book now that I'm done, I'm not entirely sure if I would have finished it had I not been reading it for a book club. <br /><br />Flannery O'Connor once described the South as "Christ haunted," and there's an element of that in this book, particularly in the last chapter – that undercurrent of holy longing and those hints of redemption just beneath the surface of a darkly broken and fallen world. Rebecca Wells doesn't capture this as powerfully as the great Southern writers (O'Connor, Faulkner, etc.), but she toys with it. She gets it in bits and pieces, here and there, then loses it, then gets it again. I think in the end the whole God as "her" thing killed it a little bit for me. It's just hard to come off as literarily serious and Christ-haunted once you go there. Yes, I get that the narrator's mother was a mess and thus God-as-mother. But…eh…it just didn't work well for me. It was distracting. It took me a little bit out of the sad beauty just as I was finally going in to it. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9706277786737138631">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/21414817-cammie-bishop" class="left imgcol" title="Cammie Bishop"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Cammie Bishop" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/657452905?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 02, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/21414817-cammie-bishop" class="user" title="Cammie Bishop">Cammie Bishop</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer657452905">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14327301330471664794">This book is worth reading. I like all the child characters and the maid, Willetta. I find it has some interesting metaphors. For example, the characters try to fix their deeply sad and disturbed psyches with obsessive religious rituals and pills and alcohol throughout the story, and then it casually mentions that at the same time they were chasing DDT trucks and covering themselves in this dangerous poison to keep the bugs away. I like that book is written from the point of views of many differ</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14327301330471664794">This book is worth reading. I like all the child characters and the maid, Willetta. I find it has some interesting metaphors. For example, the characters try to fix their deeply sad and disturbed psyches with obsessive religious rituals and pills and alcohol throughout the story, and then it casually mentions that at the same time they were chasing DDT trucks and covering themselves in this dangerous poison to keep the bugs away. I like that book is written from the point of views of many different characters. The changing view point not only kept my attention but it also showed how differently different people can be affected by the same situation. Even each child in the Walker family naturally has a different take on growing up in this atmosphere. It is also interesting to see each character speaking through their own cultural perspective. Each character has an individual voice, but they also go through the perspectives of black, white, Catholic, Baptist, male, female, child, adult, rich, and poor. I find the book well-written and thoughtful. I found very little of it funny though. It doesn't need to be funny to be a good book. However, I have noticed that it has been described that way and I would not recommend it to anyone who is just looking for a laugh, even with a dark sense of humor. The book is horribly sad in many places, and I do not find the character Vivi to be remotely likable or sympathetic. In fact, I find her downright evil and think that reconciling with her is not in Siddalee's best interest. She sort of approaches the reader the way she does her children, trying to force them to accept all the horrible things she says and does with a mixture of charm and self pity. Again, this doesn't mean the book is bad, just that some of the promotions are misleading.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14327301330471664794">...more</a>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/160747-tiffany" class="left imgcol" title="Tiffany"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1193626610p2/160747.jpg" alt="Tiffany" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/171740967?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 28, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/160747-tiffany" class="user" title="Tiffany">Tiffany</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer171740967">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9745050228109937641">A prequel to the Divine Secrets of the Ya Ya Sisterhood, this novel is a portrait of Siddalee Walker's childhood upbringing in a dysfunctional family. The novel is told from multiple different perspectives, including Sidda, her father, sister, brothers, and hired help. Rebecca Wells is a great story-teller for sure, and it was easy for me to slip into the emotional world of this novel. Having the novel told from different perspectives was also interesting, as it presented the secrets within the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9745050228109937641">A prequel to the Divine Secrets of the Ya Ya Sisterhood, this novel is a portrait of Siddalee Walker's childhood upbringing in a dysfunctional family. The novel is told from multiple different perspectives, including Sidda, her father, sister, brothers, and hired help. Rebecca Wells is a great story-teller for sure, and it was easy for me to slip into the emotional world of this novel. Having the novel told from different perspectives was also interesting, as it presented the secrets within the family. That being said, my overall reaction to the novel is pretty negative. I felt like I was vicariously traumatized for no good reason. I can't help but compare this novel to another Wells novel that I just finished, The Crowning Glory of Calla Lily Ponder. Difficult, traumatic things happen in that novel, too - but there's also a lot of love and healing. In Little Altars, there's really not any healing to speak of. In fact, seeing the children as adults was even more depressing than seeing them as children. Maybe things will round out in The Divine Secrets. I don't know. As a stand alone, I really wouldn't recommend this book to any of my friends.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9745050228109937641">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7554582837188740663">Easy read. I liked the writing and the fact that the chapters are from various characters' points of view. But why is so much literature and drama centered on people who are really messed up? I mean, we're all messed up in our own special ways, but there's such a lot of real dysfunction in fiction. In this book, the mother, Vivi, is a narcissistic alcoholic who sexually abuses and beats her children. The father, also a heavy drinker, struggles with constant feelings of inadequacy and is absent m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7554582837188740663">Easy read. I liked the writing and the fact that the chapters are from various characters' points of view. But why is so much literature and drama centered on people who are really messed up? I mean, we're all messed up in our own special ways, but there's such a lot of real dysfunction in fiction. In this book, the mother, Vivi, is a narcissistic alcoholic who sexually abuses and beats her children. The father, also a heavy drinker, struggles with constant feelings of inadequacy and is absent much of the time. The kids all suffer from various neuroses. But I suppose if you wrote about a family without dysfunctions like this it just wouldn't be as intriguing and shocking, would it? Anyway, I enjoyed the book, thought the 1950s southern dialect was fun and may even read more Rebecca Wells books in the future.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_30013410">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30013410" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1337414-carmen" class="left imgcol" title="Carmen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1259360532p2/1337414.jpg" alt="Carmen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1397493380">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1397493380" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/46881762-babs" class="left imgcol" title="Babs"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1441983066p2/46881762.jpg" alt="Babs" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1397493380?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 21, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/46881762-babs" class="user" title="Babs">Babs</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/46881762-babs?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/46881762-babs?shelf=3-star" class="actionLinkLite">3-star</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/46881762-babs?shelf=read-2006" class="actionLinkLite">read-2006</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1397493380">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3923892378884291571">I've just finished Little Altars Everywhere and it wasn't at all the book I thought it was. I picked it up thinking it was a pink and fluffy book - probably because my impressions of Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood are of a light film with Sandra Bullock dancing around a room (the only clip of the film I remember seeing). Knowing this was a prequel to "Divine Secrets I assumed, when I picked it up it was a light-hearted read as well.<br /><br />It's not at all an easy read. This book is essentially a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3923892378884291571">I've just finished Little Altars Everywhere and it wasn't at all the book I thought it was. I picked it up thinking it was a pink and fluffy book - probably because my impressions of Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood are of a light film with Sandra Bullock dancing around a room (the only clip of the film I remember seeing). Knowing this was a prequel to "Divine Secrets I assumed, when I picked it up it was a light-hearted read as well.<br /><br />It's not at all an easy read. This book is essentially about the lives of a family - mum and dad, Vivianne and "Big Shep" Walker", and their four children, Siddalee, Little Shep, Lulu and Baylor - living in the deep south in the USA. The parents are both alcoholics and mistreat the children in a terrible manner. The parents' misbehaviour has a huge impact on all four children, driving them to obsessive behaviour to cope with their treatment.<br /><br />The first half of the book is quite light-hearted and the reader is lulled into thinking the behaviour of the parents is explainable. However the second half of the book is much darker and more disturbing, and much more upsetting. It touches on sexual abuse, alcoholism, obsessive compulsive disorder, religious obsession and more.<br /><br />The book is written from all six Walker family members' perspectives, plus those of their "hired help", Chaney and Willetta, who live a short distance away. Wells as an author is very skilled at lending very distinct voices to each of the different narrators which really helps this book flow.<br /><br />It wasn't the book I expected, but all the same I enjoyed the read. I'm not quite ready to pick Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood from Mt Toobie just yet, though. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3923892378884291571">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/972487926?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 19, 2014</a>

        rated it

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer972487926">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10674377636248878651">I had read Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood a few years ago, and just recently found this book at a local library sale. It was written BEFORE the aforementioned title, so the characters were familiar to me. The first few chapters were pretty clunky and off, but once the author warmed up, the book began to flow, culminating in some incredibly emotional, endearing final chapters. It reads almost like a collection of short stories about the characters who are all inter-connected, with differe</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10674377636248878651">I had read Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood a few years ago, and just recently found this book at a local library sale. It was written BEFORE the aforementioned title, so the characters were familiar to me. The first few chapters were pretty clunky and off, but once the author warmed up, the book began to flow, culminating in some incredibly emotional, endearing final chapters. It reads almost like a collection of short stories about the characters who are all inter-connected, with different chapters dedicated to different characters' perspectives. Wells has a comfortable, quirky southern style which is delightful and makes me crave sweet iced tea, but her overuse of exclamation marks drove me around the bend! (!!!) I don't remember if the Ya-Ya book's editor fixed that problem for her and this was just an early writer issue, but I kept wanting to give her a call and remind her that she should use WORDS to convey excitement, not punctuation. Despite this annoying quirk, the book was mainly an enjoyable quick read with some jagged edges.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10674377636248878651">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_948968304">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/948968304" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9642020-max" class="left imgcol" title="Max"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1400614459p2/9642020.jpg" alt="Max" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/948968304?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 26, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9642020-max" class="user" title="Max">Max</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer948968304">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18064775864330946058">Southern women writers always capture my interest. I have taken several college courses dealing with Southern authors, but only one dealing with Southern women writers. I wish I would have been able to have taken more but I never was at a university that offered more. Carson McCullers, Eudora Welty, Harper Lee (yeah, I know she only wrote To Kill A Mockingbird, but what a book!!); one Southern woman writer that never came up when I was taking the class was Rebecca Wells. It is because she is too</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18064775864330946058">Southern women writers always capture my interest. I have taken several college courses dealing with Southern authors, but only one dealing with Southern women writers. I wish I would have been able to have taken more but I never was at a university that offered more. Carson McCullers, Eudora Welty, Harper Lee (yeah, I know she only wrote To Kill A Mockingbird, but what a book!!); one Southern woman writer that never came up when I was taking the class was Rebecca Wells. It is because she is too modern. What a shame, because I would have loved to have dissected her three books: Little Altars Everywhere, Divine Secrets of the Ya-Ya Sisterhood, and Ya-Yas In Bloom. <br /><br />The first, Little Altars Everywhere, consists of stories told from the eyes of Siddalee, Big Shep, Baylor, Viviane, Little Shep, Lulu, Willeta and Chaney (the last two being the plantation overseer and maid for the family that have been there since before the kids were born.) Siddalee, the oldest Walker child, is wise beyond her years; Big Shep Walker is married to Viviane Walker, parents to Siddalee, Baylor, Little Shep and Lulu. Each child tells a story from their point of view that belies their collective dysfunction. This is by no means a normal family, folks; but just what, exactly, IS a normal family? They are well-to-do, living on a plantation in central Louisiana; their parents friends are all well-to-do; the children attend parochial school and are all indulged beyond belief. Mama drinks to excess and Daddy is distant; sounds normal to me. <br /><br />Her first attempt at writing, Rebecca Wells puts the fun in dysfunctional with humor, horror and love. We all can find a piece of ourselves in her writings. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18064775864330946058">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_192962267">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/192962267" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5282589-barksdale-penick" class="left imgcol" title="Barksdale Penick"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" alt="Barksdale Penick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/192962267?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 01, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5282589-barksdale-penick" class="user" title="Barksdale Penick">Barksdale Penick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer192962267">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7148411874245770258">This collection of related vignettes tells the story of a family in rural Lousiana. It starts in a humorous tone, with tales of the mother and friends (the Ya Yas), but turns serious, perhaps a little too abruptly. It has one of the best stories I have ever read--the chapater about Looking for Mules, which mixes strands of aging, poinancy, and lost opportunities. Really wonderful. I strongly recommend this book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/938181456?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 15, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/20062577-fran" class="user" title="Fran">Fran</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6697.Little_Altars_Everywhere" title="Paperback 9780060759964" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/20062577-fran?shelf=literary-challenge-2014" class="actionLinkLite">literary-challenge-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer938181456">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1565732271174859109">This book of vignettes describes events in the lives of family members across the generations. Unlike other writers who have attempted the genre, Rebecca Wells does this correctly. Her narratives are rich in description involving all five senses, another technique which few authors are able to pull off successfully. That's where the positive things about this novel end.<br /><br />All of the characters are human but none of them are likable. One is hard pressed to find a single character with which to empat</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1565732271174859109">This book of vignettes describes events in the lives of family members across the generations. Unlike other writers who have attempted the genre, Rebecca Wells does this correctly. Her narratives are rich in description involving all five senses, another technique which few authors are able to pull off successfully. That's where the positive things about this novel end.<br /><br />All of the characters are human but none of them are likable. One is hard pressed to find a single character with which to empathize and some of them are downright despicable. I wanted to throw up after reading the graphic descriptions of the beatings and the thinly veiled suggestions of molestation. I can't consider the book a waste of time because it is beautifully written, but it's not something I would ever read again.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1565732271174859109">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/1624668-lauren" class="user" title="Lauren">Lauren</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1624668-lauren?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1089507327">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16574356397772959200">I really enjoyed the way the story was told and the very different characters of the children. Even before it was clear what was going on, I found Part One a little odd, like there was a missing part. The parents seemed so childish and irresponsible regarding their children. The missing link became clear in Part Two. Reading in dialogue took some getting used to, particularly the chapters from Willetta and Chaney.<br /><br />I really liked the quote toward the end:<br />"I will love you, little baby, I will do my</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16574356397772959200">I really enjoyed the way the story was told and the very different characters of the children. Even before it was clear what was going on, I found Part One a little odd, like there was a missing part. The parents seemed so childish and irresponsible regarding their children. The missing link became clear in Part Two. Reading in dialogue took some getting used to, particularly the chapters from Willetta and Chaney.<br /><br />I really liked the quote toward the end:<br />"I will love you, little baby, I will do my best to protect you from harm. I will not be able to stop you from suffering, but I will do my best to protect you from deliberate cruelty."<br /><br />It's so true. We should do the best we can but suffering is a part of life and we can't protect from everything, even as it breaks our hearts. I'm looking forward to reading Ya-Ya Sisterhood, I think.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('aZwYrpRoFW+c7+isoEZezfrbg5fuuJeZCm2lUqBH6SsMSCd9ZroBlRA3nBfeJ7h/syxAjqdl0DNgZKDAbNIfbg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZTWpCwBRSXgfFqRAxc43dLJR1sfG1honaRENOn0U71sA4ZbY8oNdgpPO0Pu7r9HG+6YV3o8LXY0DGAiosYEZHg==')})
  }
//]]&gt;

Why should Uncle Stephen send it to me? The Remember Box was Aunt Kate\'s private place, the one we were sternly forbidden to open. Suddenly I was reluctant, even fearful--a modern Pandora, about to let out our own lost world. That box held one year I\'d spent a lifetime trying to forget.\n\nSummer in Job\'s Corner meant big trees, cool grass, and sweltering afternoons stretching endlessly under the Southern sun. Those were the days without plastic, microwaves, television, or air conditioning, a time when clocks ticked comfortingly in the night and a cool breeze was a gift. But as the long sultry summer of 1949 comes to an end, events will transform this sleepy Southern crossroads.\n\nAfter losing her mother to polio, eleven-year-old Carley Marshall comes to Job\'s Corner to make a new start, along with her Aunt Kate and Uncle Stephen Whitfield and her cousins Abby and John. The family is welcomed warmly by this small North Carolina community as Stephen takes up the post of pastor to Bethel Church, a Presbyterian congregation. But their welcome begins to wear thin and covert criticism runs rampant as Stephen challenges age-old beliefs and traditions.\n\nAs Job\'s Corner confronts national struggles for civil rights, coal strikes, and hysteria over Communism, Stephen\'s voice of reason gets lost in the growing hostility of a vocal minority. Though this quintessential Southern community seems to be filled with people who are the salt of the earth, secrets and lies are hidden beneath the easy-going surface--and the truth must be revealed before an innocent man is convicted of murder.\n\nWith the dawning of a new decade, Carley learns to face her own family secrets.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3489.Rebecca_Wells">About Rebecca Wells</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/3489.Rebecca_Wells"><img alt="3489" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1246916138p2/3489.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer59752004446919102">Rebecca Wells was born and raised in Alexandria, Louisiana. “I grew up,” she says, “in the fertile world of story-telling, filled with flamboyance, flirting, futility, and fear.” Surrounded by Louisiana raconteurs, a large extended family, and Our Lady of Prompt Succor’s Parish, Rebecca’s imagination was stimulated at every turn. Early on, she fell in love with thinking up and acting in plays for</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText59752004446919102">Rebecca Wells was born and raised in Alexandria, Louisiana. “I grew up,” she says, “in the fertile world of story-telling, filled with flamboyance, flirting, futility, and fear.” Surrounded by Louisiana raconteurs, a large extended family, and Our Lady of Prompt Succor’s Parish, Rebecca’s imagination was stimulated at every turn. Early on, she fell in love with thinking up and acting in plays for her siblings—the beginnings of her career as an actress and writer for the stage. She recalls her early influences as being the land around her, harvest times, craw-fishing in the bayou, practicing piano after school, dancing with her mother and brothers and sister, and the close relationship to her black “mother” who cleaned for the Wells household. She counts black music and culture from Louisiana as something that will stay in her body’s memory forever.<br /><br />In high school, she read Walt Whitman’s “I Sing the Body Electric,” which opened her up to the idea that everything in life is a poem, and that, as she says, “We are not born separately from one another.” She also read “Howl,” Allen Ginsberg’s indictment of the strangling consumer-driven American culture he saw around him. Acting in school and summer youth theater productions freed Rebecca to step out of the social hierarchies of high school and into the joys of walking inside another character and living in another world.<br /><br />The day after she graduated from high school, Rebecca left for Yellowstone National Park, where she worked as a waitress. It was an introduction to the natural glories of the park—mountains, waterfalls, hot springs, and geysers—as well as to the art of hitchhiking.<br /><br />Rebecca graduated from Louisiana State University (LSU) in Baton Rouge, where she studied theater, English, and psychology. She performed in many college plays, but also stepped outside the theater department to become awakened to women’s politics. During this time she worked as a cocktail waitress--once accidentally kicking a man in the shins when he slipped a ten-dollar bill down the front of her dress—and began keeping a journal after reading Anais Nin, which she has done ever since.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.rebeccawellsbooks.com/about/">Click here to read more...</a><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="59752004446919102">...more</a>

    </div>
    <a href="/author/show/3489.Rebecca_Wells" class="actionLink moreLink">More about Rebecca Wells...</a>

</a>
